    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="1c8fd72734874f799b05c4452787d2eb">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'd6171650-3ecf-4771-b814-8bfbea6267b8'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d38803414" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>MXene is investigated as an electrode material for different energy storage systems due to layered structures and metal-like electrical conductivity. Experimental results show MXenes possess excellent cycling performance as anode materials, especially at large current densities. However, the reversible capacity is relatively low, which is a significant barrier to meeting the demands of industrial applications. This work synthesizes N-doped graphene-like carbon (NGC) intercalated Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> (NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>) van der Waals heterostructure by an in situ method. The as-prepared NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> van der Waals heterostructure is employed as sodium-ion and lithium-ion battery electrodes. For sodium-ion batteries, a reversible specific capacity of 305&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is achieved at a specific current of 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, 2.3 times higher than that of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>. For lithium-ion batteries, a reversible capacity of 400&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a specific current of 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is 1.5 times higher than that of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>. Both sodium-ion and lithium-ion batteries made from NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> shows high cycling stability. The theoretical calculations also verify the remarkable improvement in battery capacity within the NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> system, attributed to the additional adsorption of working ions at the edge states of NGC. This work offers an innovative way to synthesize a new van der Waals heterostructure and provides a new route to improve the electrochemical performance significantly.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs8518-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs8518-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Since transition metal carbides and/or nitrides, MXenes, were discovered in 2011,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0001_R_d38803402e612" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> their number has increased rapidly, and more than 50 different compositions have been synthesized to date.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0001_R_d38803402e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#advs8518-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0004_R_d38803402e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MXenes show outstanding physical, chemical, electronic, and optical properties, giving rise to their use in many applications from electrochemical energy storage<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0005_R_d38803402e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">5</a>, <a href="#advs8518-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0006_R_d38803402e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Sci.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> to catalysis,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0007_R_d38803402e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - ACS Energy Lett.">7</a>, <a href="#advs8518-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0008_R_d38803402e628" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> electronics,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0009_R_d38803402e633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Energy Lett.">9</a>-<a href="#advs8518-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0011_R_d38803402e633" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> sensing,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0012_R_d38803402e639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Sci. Adv.">12</a>-<a href="#advs8518-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0014_R_d38803402e639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Carbon Energy">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and bio-related applications. Owing to its layered structure and metal-like electrical conductivity (≈24 000&nbsp;S&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0015_R_d38803402e646" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Nanoscale">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MXenes have been investigated as electrode materials for many different electrochemical energy storage systems, such as supercapacitors,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0016_R_d38803402e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">16</a>, <a href="#advs8518-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0017_R_d38803402e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Energy Storage">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> lithium-ion batteries (LIBs),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0018_R_d38803402e657" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Small">18</a>, <a href="#advs8518-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0019_R_d38803402e657" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Small Methods">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> beyond LIBs (Na<sup>+</sup>, K<sup>+</sup>, Mg<sup>2+</sup>, Ca<sup>2+</sup>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0020_R_d38803402e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Nano">20</a>-<a href="#advs8518-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0023_R_d38803402e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and alkali ion-capacitors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0024_R_d38803402e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">24</a>, <a href="#advs8518-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0025_R_d38803402e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - InfoMat">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MXenes exhibit very high rate handling capabilities (charge and discharge at high current densities), which is crucial for fast-charging battery systems.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0026_R_d38803402e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Chem. Eng. J.">26</a>, <a href="#advs8518-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0027_R_d38803402e682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Appl. Nano Mater">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the reversible capacity is relatively low especially for multilayer MXenes, which is a significant barrier to meet for industrial use. For example, as sodium-ion battery (SIB) electrodes, Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>, Ti<sub>3</sub>CNT<i><sub>x</sub></i>, and Ti<sub>2</sub>C<sub>0.5</sub>N<sub>0.5</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> show specific capacities of 103, 157, and 182&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, at a rate of 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> electrolyte in ethylene carbonate (EC)/diethyl carbonate (DEC) with 1:1 (by volume).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0021_R_d38803402e718" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - InfoMat">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Three approaches are usually explored to improve the charge storage capacity of MXene electrodes: oxidation of MXenes, introduction of external metal ions, and making MXene composites. i) Oxidation of MXenes: Metal oxides, such as TiO<sub>2</sub>, Nb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, and mixed-metal oxides (like titanium niobium oxide<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0019_R_d38803402e731" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Small Methods">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>), are promising anode candidates for rechargeable batteries owing to their high capacity and low cost. 2D Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> can be oxidized in air to synthesize TiO<sub>2</sub> nanocrystals enmeshed in thin sheets of disordered graphitic carbon structures, which can handle very high cycling rates as LIBs anode material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0004_R_d38803402e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> ii) Introducing external metal ions: Sn(IV)-modified/decorated Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> and V<sub>2</sub>CT<i><sub>x</sub></i> were reported to achieve high specific capacities of 635 and 1284&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a rate of 100&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0028_R_d38803402e769" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - ACS Nano">28</a>, <a href="#advs8518-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0029_R_d38803402e769" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Energy Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> iii) MXene composites: Arnold et al presented a guideline for designing an advanced hybrid antimony MXene compound for application in high-performance SIBs, with a high reversible capacity of 450 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>&nbsp;at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>&nbsp;with a capacity retention of ≈96% after 100 cycles.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0030_R_d38803402e779" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Mater. Chem. A">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All the methods mentioned above improved the capacity; however, the cycling stability of the as-prepared electrode was decreased. Therefore, a balance between high capacity and excellent cycling stability should be a goal for future research.</p>
         
         <p>Considering the intercalation/deintercalation mechanism of MXene-based energy storage, theoretical studies show that two layers of Li-ions can be absorbed on the MXene surfaces, which allows fast ion diffusion leading to high capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0030_R_d38803402e787" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Mater. Chem. A">30</a>, <a href="#advs8518-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0031_R_d38803402e787" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - J. Phys. Chem. C">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Theoretical studies predict that two layers of Li-ions can be adsorbed, but in experimental practice, the stoichiometry does not reach this level.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0020_R_d38803402e792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Nano">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We hypothesize that intercalating MXene with another layer of electrically conductive material will allow for two alkali ions layers between the MXene sheets instead of one layer of alkali ions. Recently, we proved that the interlayer spacings could be tuned by intercalating polymeric chains with different chain lengths.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0032_R_d38803402e797" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, MXene heterostructures might be a good option to achieve high capacity with excellent cycling stability. Very recently, an in situ carbon intercalated Nb<sub>2</sub>CT<i><sub>x</sub></i> was reported to achieve significantly improved electrochemical performance as a potassium-ion battery electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0033_R_d38803402e808" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chem. Eng. J.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Herein, we report an in situ method to synthesize an N-doped graphene-like carbon intercalated Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> (NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>) heterostructure and evaluate the electrochemical performance as SIBs and LIBs. For SIBs, a reversible specific capacity of 305&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> was achieved at a specific current of 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, more than twice that of pristine Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene. For LIBs, a reversible capacity of 400&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a specific current of 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is 1.5 times higher than that of pristine Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene. Both SIBs and LIBs show excellent cycling stability. In addition, theoretical calculations were performed to understand the capacity increase further. The maximum Na-ion concentration in NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is 2.3 (maximum number of Na per unit cell), with a corresponding capacity of 289&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is also close to the experimental result (≈305&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) for Na-ion intercalation. The extra Na-ions could also adsorb on the edge sites around the NGC, accounting for a higher capacity than theoretical calculations predict. Similarly, the maximum Li-ion concentration in NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is 3.0 (maximum number of Li per unit cell) with the corresponding capacity of 371&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is also close to the experimental result (≈400&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) for Li-ion intercalation.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs8518-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs8518-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8518-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs8518-sec-0030-title">2.1 Materials Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The process to prepare the NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> heterostructure material is schematically illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0001">1a</a>: i) A mixed solution of HF and LiCl was used to remove Al from Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> powders and intercalate Li<sup>+</sup> between the layers of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene, producing Li-intercalated Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXenes; ii) ion exchange from Li<sup>+</sup> to protonated dopamine by immersing Li-intercalated Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXenes into dopamine hydrochloride solution, leading to dopamine cations intercalation in-between the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> layers, yielding dopamine-intercalated Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> (D-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>) MXenes; iii) annealing the as-prepared D-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXenes at 600&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h under Ar gas, to finally produce NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXenes. This temperature was chosen to ensure dopamine is decomposed to form NGC without causing recrystallizing Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0034_R_d38803402e978" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Polym. Chem.">34</a>-<a href="#advs8518-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0037_R_d38803402e978" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Appl. Phys. Lett.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), the MAX phase peaks disappeared, and the MXenes peaks can be observed clearly, reflecting the successful removal of Al from Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> powders and conversion of the MAX phase into MXene. The successful intercalation of dopamine can be evaluated by monitoring the (002) peak position of MXene to determine the <i>d</i>-spacing of D-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> and NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXenes in comparison to pristine and annealed MXene. The <i>d</i>-spacing can be calculated using Bragg's law, <i>d</i>&nbsp; = &nbsp;λ/2sinθ, where λ is the X-ray wavelength (λ&nbsp; = &nbsp;0.1545&nbsp;nm) and θ is the diffraction angle. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0001">1b</a>, the <i>d</i>-spacing shifts from 13.4&nbsp;Å (6.6°&nbsp;2θ) for pristine Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXenes to 15.6&nbsp;Å (5.7°&nbsp;2θ) after the dopamine intercalation, which means the interlayer spacing expands by ≈2.2&nbsp;Å, indicating that dopamine cations are intercalated in-between the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene layers. Compared to Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene after annealing, as displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0001">1c</a>, we can calculate that the <i>d</i>-spacing of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene is 12.8&nbsp;Å, which is ≈2.6&nbsp;Å larger than annealed Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>. The reduction in the <i>d</i>-spacing upon annealing is not surprising, considering that in the temperature range of 100–600&nbsp;°C, the removal of intercalated water and -OH termination groups bonding to Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub> MXene leads to decreased <i>d</i>-space.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0038_R_d38803402e1071" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - ACS Energy Lett.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs8518-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9da393a0-71f1-4ff9-8383-ba082c0f5551/advs8518-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9da393a0-71f1-4ff9-8383-ba082c0f5551/advs8518-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6858fd7c-83f3-4fe8-a894-fc82fd0feadc/advs8518-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9da393a0-71f1-4ff9-8383-ba082c0f5551/advs8518-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs8518-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202402708" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Synthesis process and material characterization of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> samples. a) A schematic illustration of the preparation of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>. X-ray diffractograms of pristine Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> and D-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> b) before and c) after annealing at 600&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h. d) Raman spectra of various samples. e,f) HAADF-STEM images of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> material and g) corresponding intensity line scan showing the interlayer spacing.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The Raman spectrum of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene (bottom spectrum in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0001">1d</a>) shows peaks at 210, 375, 580, and 740&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, which are slightly different from Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene after HF treatment,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0003_R_d38803402e1155" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#advs8518-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0004_R_d38803402e1155" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Sci.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> because of the different surface terminations.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0019_R_d38803402e1161" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Small Methods">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> All Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene Raman peaks can be observed in annealed Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>, D-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub>, and NGC- Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXenes in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0001">1d</a>. In D-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>, dopamine peaks can be found at 653, 1326, and 1480&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup>, consistent with the reported dopamine Raman data.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0039_R_d38803402e1208" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Biosensors">39</a>, <a href="#advs8518-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0040_R_d38803402e1208" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Int. J. Nanomed.">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> XRD and Raman data confirm that dopamine was successfully intercalated between the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene sheets. Raman spectrum of as synthesized MXene (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0001">1d</a>) shows the characteristic D-band and G-band of incompletely graphitic carbon. This may be related to carbon impurities in the MAX phases or from over-etching MXene leading to the small amount of carbide-derived carbon. The D and G peaks of annealed MXene sample showed a blueshift and decreased width, indicating an increased degree of graphitization. The reasons for this graphitization are currently not clear and warrant further investigations. Considering that graphitization of amorphous carbon typically requires much higher temperatures, this suggests that MXene might act as a catalyst to lower the graphitization temperature of carbon. This observation can be explained that during annealing, the surface terminations (OH, F, O groups) on the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene sheets decompose and are removed. This leaves behind a carbon-rich surface. The carbon atoms originally within the MXene structure become freer to rearrange without the steric hindrance of surface terminations. The thermal energy provided by annealing allows the carbon atoms to gradually reorder into more extended conjugated aromatic clusters and domains within the MXene sheets. Defects within the original carbon lattice are gradually eliminated through a recrystallization process driven by the annealing. This increased degree of graphitization leads to the blueshift and sharpening of the D-band and G-band peaks observed in the Raman spectra after annealing Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene. In addition, the D-band and G-band intensity ratio, I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub>, of annealed dopamine, annealed Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>, and NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXenes are estimated as 0.94, 0.80, and 0.88, respectively, indicating the existence of disordered graphitic carbon in annealed dopamine and NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXenes. Furthermore, the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> of 0.88 in NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene aligns with the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> value of 0.9 reported for stacked MoS<sub>2</sub> and N-doped graphene.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0041_R_d38803402e1284" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Considering the high I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio, possibly due to highly disordered carbon, but still the nanoconfined nature of the carbon between MXene layers, we have used “graphene-like” to describe the intercalated carbon species. Exploring oxygen-free carbon sources as intercalants might lead to a more ordered carbon confined between the layers.</p>
            
            <p>The NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene structure was studied using high-angle annular dark-field (HAADF) scanning transmission electron microscopy&nbsp;(STEM) imaging, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0001">1e,g</a>. Low and high-magnification STEM images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0001">1e,f</a> reveals the typical layered Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> structure. The interlayer spacing between NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene layers was measured to be ≈1.2&nbsp;nm using line intensity profile measurements from the region marked in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0001">1e,f</a>, which agrees with the XRD results (1.28&nbsp;nm). However, we still can observe that the interlayer spacings were not the same for different locations due to the different morphologies of intercalated nanoflakes. During the annealing process, the intercalated dopamine cations acted as carbon and nitrogen sources and decomposed in their respective locations within the confined MXene interspace. The decomposed products (viz., C and N atoms) may segregate and precipitate out as nanoflakes due to their confinement and limited solubility in MXene. The MXene surface or defects/edges may catalyze the localized graphitization of intercalated C and N precursors, leading to nanoflaked morphology rather than a homogeneous intercalation compound. Additionally, differences in thermal expansion between MXene layers and intercalated species could induce internal stresses during annealing, driving the nanoflaked self-assembly to relieve stress.</p>
            
            <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurements were carried out to investigate chemical states of the elements of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene. From the XPS survey shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2a</a> (Supporting Information), Ti, C, and N were detected with surface terminations containing O and F. The weak Al peak demonstrates the removal of Al to form multilayer Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene. After analyzing the high-resolution elements spectra, as displayed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0002">2a</a>, Ti 2p peak can be deconvoluted to +1, +2, +3, and +4 oxidation states, corresponding to -O/-OH/-F terminations. 11% photoemission of Ti-ON is noted in the Ti 2p peak, indicating N can terminate the MXene surfaces when dopamine is decomposed during the annealing treatment. High-resolution N 1s region spectra show four fitted peaks at 396.4, 398.4, 399.9, and 401.0&nbsp;eV, which correspond to Ti-ON and pyridinic/pyrrolic/graphitic N from N-doped carbon species, respectively. 43% photoemission of Ti-ON is found in the N 1s peak (Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information), confirming the existence of -ON termination. The fractions of pyridinic/pyrrolic/graphitic N are 40%, 12%, and 6%, respectively. Large amounts of pyridinic and pyrrolic N fractions indicate most of the N are located at the edge or surface hole defect sites in a graphene-like carbon layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0042_R_d38803402e1359" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nano Lett.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As presented in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0002">2c</a>, the C 1s spectrum can be fitted by four peaks, including a C─N peak at 286.4&nbsp;eV with 3% photoemission, demonstrating the existence of N-doped carbon species. All of the above supports that we have synthesized the NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXenes. For the O1s region in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0002">2d</a>, we can observe the Ti-ON or Ti-OF terminations. As presented in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2b</a> (Supporting Information) shows both Ti─F and Ti-OF terminations. More XPS details, including peak positions, full width at half maximum, FWHM, and fractions, are summarized in Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs8518-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ad621721-7484-4bff-b16f-55836b44268f/advs8518-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ad621721-7484-4bff-b16f-55836b44268f/advs8518-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f30a66a7-cdcd-49f5-aef1-0680f41c1cd5/advs8518-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ad621721-7484-4bff-b16f-55836b44268f/advs8518-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs8518-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202402708" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XPS analysis of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>. a,d) High-resolution XPS spectra of Ti 2p, N 1s, C1s, O1s in NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>.</div>
                  </figcaption>

            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8518-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs8518-sec-0040-title">2.2 Sodium-Ion Battery Application</h3>
            
            <p>To investigate the electrochemical energy storage performance of as-prepared NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene, standard CR2032 SIBs configurations were assembled with NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene as working electrode and Na foil as counter/reference electrodes in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub>/EC-DEC electrolyte. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0003">3a</a> shows the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene during the first four cycles at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> between 0.01 and 3.0&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. Three broad peaks located at ≈0.4, 1.25, and 2.1&nbsp;V are exhibited in the first cathodic scan, but they disappear in the following cycles; therefore, they can be ascribed to the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI), and/or irreversible reactions with surface terminations. The cyclic voltammograms almost overlap from the second to the fourth cycle, indicating a highly reversible electrochemical behavior. For the first cycle, the cyclic voltammogram shape of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0003">3a</a>) MXene is different from the one of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting NGC plays an essential role in the electrochemical performance of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene. Compared to the cyclic voltammogram of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene, the peaks at 0.4&nbsp;V and 2.1&nbsp;V are observed in both materials, while the peak at ≈1.25&nbsp;V is pronounced in NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> only; this can be assigned to either -ON terminated MXene or the NGC, indicated by its undeniable portion in the N 1s spectra. For cycles beyond the first, three pairs of redox peaks are noted at 0.31/0.40, 1.28/1.43, and 1.80/1.95&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> for NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene, while there are no pronounced redox peaks for Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs8518-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f39e3be1-45fb-4cc0-890e-4f4794ae8fad/advs8518-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f39e3be1-45fb-4cc0-890e-4f4794ae8fad/advs8518-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/606fc41a-9fdf-41ee-8fc5-94ee820b846d/advs8518-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f39e3be1-45fb-4cc0-890e-4f4794ae8fad/advs8518-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs8518-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202402708" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> as a Na-ion battery electrode material. a) Cyclic voltammograms of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> for the first four cycles at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Galvanostatic charge/discharge testing of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> for the first ten cycles at a specific current of 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Rate capabilities of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> as Na-ion battery electrodes. d) Nyquist plots NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>. Inset is the equivalent circuit. The dots are the original data, and the solid line are the fitting curves. e) Long-term cycling performance at a current density of 200&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>After NGC intercalation, the interlayer spacing of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> increases by ≈0.6 Å compared with Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. This provides additional space to store Na-ions, thereby playing a crucial vital in enhancing the capacity of Na-ion battery. Additionally, the existence of -ON termination has been confirmed in the N 1s peak, which contributes to the formation of an additional three redox sites of reactions. This is further demonstrated by the appearance of three pairs of redox peaks at 0.31/0.40, 1.28/1.43, and 1.80/1.95&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> for NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene, while no pronounced redox peaks are observed for Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene. Furthermore, as reported, N-groups exhibit the sodiophilicity, and thus absorbing more Na-ions,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0043_R_d38803402e1639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Nano-Micro Lett.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which corresponds with the calculated results, that is, a maximum of 2.3 Na per NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> unit cell.</p>
            
            <p>Galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) voltage profiles of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene at a specific current of 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0003">3b</a>. The initial discharge process delivers a specific capacity of 447&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which then decreases to 317&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for the first charge process, showing a Coulombic efficiency of 71%. The specific capacity fading in the first sodiation and de-sodiation steps is attributed to SEI formation and other irreversible reactions between sodium, the NGC, and/or the MXene surface terminations. A reversible specific capacity of 305&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is achieved after five cycles.</p>
            
            <p>GCD testing was conducted at different specific currents to further evaluate the electrochemical performance of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene as electrodes for SIBs. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0003">3c</a>, the NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene electrode shows reversible specific capacities of 305, 266, 243, 216, 176, and 140&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at specific currents of 20, 50, 100, 200, 500, and 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. The specific capacity increased to 286&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> when the specific current returned to 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, suggesting a high-rate handling capability and sodiation/de-sodiation reversibility. Compared to Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene, the specific capacities of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene increased 2.3-6.0 times when the specific currents increase from 20 to 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, reflecting intercalating NGC is a promising approach to increase the specific capacity, especially at high specific currents. It can be ascribed to N atoms on the intercalated NGC layers providing more Na ions absorption sites, amalgamated with expanded d-spacing for expedited ions transport.</p>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">For most electrode materials, the total charge storage encompasses a balance of surface and diffusion contributions, which varies with operating conditions and material properties. Understanding both aspects is crucial for characterizing charge storage performance. Surface-controlled processes pertain to charge storage mechanisms that occur at or near the electrode/electrolyte interface, such as double layer capacitance, pseudocapacitance, and surface redox reactions. Diffusion-controlled processes refer to charge storage mechanisms that require ion insertion/extraction into the bulk electrode material, such as intercalation reactions in batteries. The response from the peak current in curves and the scan rate can be described by a power law as follows:<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0032_R_d38803402e1724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#advs8518-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0044_R_d38803402e1724" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Small">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><div class="inline-equation" id="advs8518-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/5e475780-31c3-4f55-8f61-0fcb87d4f89f/advs8518-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:21983844:media:advs8518:advs8518-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/advs8518-math-0001.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <mrow>
                              <msub>
                                 <mi>i</mi>
                                 <mi>p</mi>
                              </msub>
                              <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                              <mi>a</mi>
                              <msup>
                                 <mi>v</mi>
                                 <mi>b</mi>
                              </msup>
                           </mrow>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{i}_p} = a{{v}^b}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> is the peak current, <i>v</i> is the scan rate, <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are adjustable parameters. Here, we can use the <i>b</i> value to reflect the rate-determining step. Specifically, <i>b</i> = 1.0 indicates a surface-controlled step, while <i>b</i> = 0.5 donates a semi-infinite diffusion-controlled step. As illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), the <i>b</i> values were calculated to be ≈0.62 for NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene, corresponding to a combined control step between surface-controlled processes and diffusion-controlled processes. In contrast, the <i>b</i> values for Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene were calculated to be ≈0.5, suggesting a mainly diffusion-controlled system. Determining the exact mechanism would require analysis of the material/system properties and could be complex. However, diffusion still plays a significant role in the rate determination.</div>
            
            <p>To investigate the kinetics of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was conducted in the frequency range from 100 to 100&nbsp;kHz. As displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0003">3d</a>, a semicircle in the high-to-medium-frequency range and a straight line in the low-frequency range can be observed in Nyquist plots of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> and NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene electrodes. To further understand the EIS, the inset in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0003">3d</a> shows an equivalent circuit, including the system resistance (R<sub>s</sub>), charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>), Warburg resistance (W), and a constant phase element (CPE). The NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene electrode shows smaller estimated system resistance (6.5&nbsp;Ω) and charge transfer resistance (16.9&nbsp;Ω) than those for the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene electrode (of 11.1–62.4&nbsp;Ω, respectively), demonstrating that intercalating NGC can facilitate the charge transfer and ion diffusion to enhance the electrochemical performance.</p>
            
            <p>As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0003">3e</a>, the as-prepared Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> and NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXenes present stable specific capacities of 85 and 256 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, after 90 cycles at a specific current of 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, suggesting excellent cycling performance after intercalating NGC nanoflakes. The NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene electrode shows higher Coulombic efficiency than Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene in the initial sodiation/de-sodiation stage, indicating highly efficient electrochemical redox processes after intercalating NGC.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8518-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs8518-sec-0050-title">2.3 Lithium-Ion Battery Application</h3>
            
            <p>We also tested NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene as an electrode for LIB application. Similar to the results from SIB testing, the electrochemical performance of LIB is improved by intercalating NGC. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5a</a> (Supporting Information), NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene electrode shows an initial discharge capacity of 504&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, and achieves a reversible capacity of 400&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after five cycles at a specific current of 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene electrode shows reversible specific capacities of 400, 350, 325, 304, 267, and 235&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at specific currents of 20, 50, 100, 200, 500, and 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. The specific capacity came back to 392&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> when the specific current returned to 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating the high-rate handling capability and lithiation/de-lithiation reversibility. Compared to Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene, the specific capacities of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> Mxene increase ≈1.5 times when the specific currents increase from 20 to 1000&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5c</a> (Supporting Information), the as-prepared NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> Mxene presents a stable specific capacity after 20 cycles at a specific current of 200&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, suggesting excellent cycling performance.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8518-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="advs8518-sec-0060-title">2.4 Li- and Na-Ion Intercalation Modeling</h3>
            
            <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were performed to understand the observed enhanced capacity. First, we constructed the structure of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6a</a> (Supporting Information), and the calculated <i>c</i>-lattice constant is 9.67&nbsp;Å. After that, various ion intercalation configurations of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> were optimized. We find that both the Li- and Na-ions could intercalate into the interlayer of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. The calculated maximum Li-concentration “x” in the stoichiometric formula Li<sub>x</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> could reach 2, much greater than the maximum Na-ion concentration of 1.125 in Na<sub>x</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. The corresponding configurations with different Li- or Na-ion concentrations are shown in Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. The capacities are estimated as 259 and 146&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for LIBs and NIBs with Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> electrodes, respectively. For comparison, the experimental capacities of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> are 200 and 130&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for LIB and NIB, respectively.</p>
            
            <p>Our calculated results are consistent well with experimental results. The much higher capacity of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> for Li-ion intercalation aligns with the smaller ionic radius of Li-ions than Na-ions. In addition, as a benefit of the NGC intercalation in the structure, the interlayer spacing of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> increases to 12.3&nbsp;Å compared to Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, as shown in&nbsp;Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6b</a> (Supporting Information). The interlayer spacing increases by ≈0.6&nbsp;Å with NGC in the interlayer, which is consistent well with the experimental result (10.2–12.8&nbsp;Å) after dopamine intercalation. This interlayer spacing increase gives NGC-T<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> more space to store the Li- or Na-ions. As shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0004">4a</a>-<a href="#advs8518-fig-0004">d</a>, the Li-ion prefers to adsorb on the layers of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with a Li-ion concentration of up to 2.0. After the complete adsorption of Li-ions on&nbsp;the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> layers, one extra Li-ion could adsorb on the edge site around the NGC. The extra Li adsorption makes the maximum Li-ion concentration x in NGC-Li<sub>x</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> reach 3.0, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0004">4e</a>, and the corresponding capacity is 371&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. This estimated capacity value is close to the experimental result of ≈400&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for dopamine-intercalated MXene.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="advs8518-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1294ecd5-8434-4c35-88f4-00edd0eb5a67/advs8518-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/1294ecd5-8434-4c35-88f4-00edd0eb5a67/advs8518-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/366d4569-bcf1-4138-b56f-d6fb381cef9c/advs8518-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1294ecd5-8434-4c35-88f4-00edd0eb5a67/advs8518-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=advs8518-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadvs.202402708" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The optimized structures of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with different Li-ion concentrations: a) NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>-0.083Li, b) NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>-0.667Li, c) NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>-1.0Li, d) NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>-2.0Li, and e) NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>-3.0Li, respectively. f) The calculated average voltage for Li- and Na-ion intercalation into NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. g) The electron-density-difference isosurfaces for Li-ion intercalation around the edge of NGC (left, side view; right, top view). Isosurface value at 0.002&nbsp;e Bohr<sup>−</sup>3: electron accumulation, yellow; electron depletion, cyan.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Similarly, extra Na-ions could also adsorb on the edge site around the NGC, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). The maximum Na-ion concentration (maximum number of Na per unit cell) in NGC-Na<sub>x</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is 2.33, with the corresponding capacity of 289&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which is also close to the experimental result (≈300&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) for Na-ion intercalation. Based on various ion intercalation configurations in NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, the calculated voltage profile is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0004">4f</a> for both Li-ion and Na-ion. The higher average voltage for Li-ion intercalation in NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> results in a higher energy density. To gain deeper insights, we plotted electron-density-difference isosurfaces during Li-ion intercalation, as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#advs8518-fig-0004">4g</a>. This visualization clearly illustrates the transfer of electrons from Li (in the cyan region) to the NGC (in the yellow region) upon introducing extra Li ions. The accumulation of electrons along the periphery of the NGC suggests that the NGC can store some of these electrons. This property enhances the performance of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> significantly when compared to Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> alone.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs8518-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs8518-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, novel NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> heterostructures were synthesized using an in situ method by carbonizing dopamine-intercalated MXene. XRD, Raman, XPS, and STEM data confirm that NGC was intercalated between the MX-layers of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>. When tested as electrodes for SIBs and LIBs, the as-prepared NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> heterostructure showed a significantly improved electrochemical performance. For SIBs, a reversible specific capacity of 305 mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is achieved at a specific current of 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, 2.3 times higher than that of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene. For LIBs, a reversible capacity of 400&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at a specific current of 20&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is 1.5 times higher than that of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> MXene. Theoretical calculations show that NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> heterostructure has more space for the Li- or Na-ion storage, and the extra Li- or Na-ions could adsorb on the edge site around the NGC. This work offers an innovative way to synthesize new heterostructures and provides a new route to improve electrochemical performance significantly.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="advs8518-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="advs8518-sec-0080-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8518-sec-0090">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8518-sec-0100">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8518-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs8518-sec-0110-title"> Synthesis of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>x</sub></h4>
                  
                  <p>Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> powders were prepared using a procedure described in the earlier work.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0032_R_d38803402e2307" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In short, Ti (99.99%,−325 mesh, Alfa Aesar), aluminum (99.5%,−325 mesh, Alfa Aesar), and graphite (99.9995%,−325 mesh, Alfa Aesar) were mixed with a molar ratio of 3.00:1.20:1.88, then transferred into an alumina crucible and heated to 1600&nbsp;°C at 10&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> for 2&nbsp;h in an alumina tube furnace under a continuous Ar flow. The samples of D-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub><i>x</i></sub> were prepared through wet chemical etching and ion exchange. Briefly, 2&nbsp;g of Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> powders (−325 mesh; &lt;44&nbsp;µm) were slowly added into 100&nbsp;mL of 10 mass% aqueous hydrofluoric acid solution (HF, 48–51%, Acros Organics) and 2&nbsp;g lithium chloride (LiCl, 99%, Fisher Scientific) in a 250&nbsp;mL high-density polyethylene bottle. The reaction temperature was maintained at 25&nbsp;°C in an oil bath for 24&nbsp;h. After that, the mixture was centrifuged at 3500&nbsp;rpm to separate the powders and solution, and then the settled powders were washed using 6&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> aqueous hydrochloride acid (HCl, 36.5–38%, Fisher Chemicals) and degassed deionized (DI) water for three times, respectively. The remaining sediments were transferred into 50&nbsp;mL of 0.5&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> dopamine hydrochloride (99%, Thermo Scientific) solution and rested for 4&nbsp;days with daily manual shaking. Finally, the intercalated MXene was washed four times with DI water, and the materials were collected and dried using vacuum-assisted filtration. The dried materials, referred to as D-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>, were transferred into a tube furnace and annealed at 600&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h with a heating rate of 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup> under a continuous flow of argon, Ar, gas at a flow rate of 100&nbsp;sccm. The resulting black powders were collected and labeled as NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>. For comparison, non-intercalated Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> after HF/LiCl etching was annealed under the same conditions.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8518-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs8518-sec-0120-title"> Materials Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>X-ray diffraction was performed using a Rigaku DMAX2200 powder diffractometer with Cu-Kα radiation (λ = 0.154&nbsp;nm) at 40&nbsp;kV and 40&nbsp;mA. The step size and scan rate were set at 0.02° 2θ and 1°min<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.</p>
                  
                  <p>XPS (Thermo Fisher) configured with an Al-K<sub>α</sub> excitation source was used to check the chemical states of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>. Before collecting the XPS spectrum, the surfaces of the samples were cleaned by Ar<sup>+</sup> sputtering for 5&nbsp;min. The data were analyzed using the CasaXPS software.</p>
                  
                  <p>Raman analysis was carried out using a Renishaw InVia microscope with a He-Ne laser operating at 632.8&nbsp;nm excitation wavelength, having a power of ≈0.1&nbsp;mW at the focal point of the sample. For each sample, spectra from ten points were recorded with 20&nbsp;s exposure time and accumulated five times. The microscope employed a 50x magnifying lens with a numeric aperture of 0.75. The samples were placed on a glass slide, and the system was calibrated with a silicon single crystal.</p>
                  
                  <p>STEM imaging was performed using the Nion UltraSTEM100, equipped with a cold-field emission gun and an aberration corrector on the probe-forming lens. HAADF STEM images were acquired at an accelerating voltage of 60&nbsp;kV, a convergence angle of 31&nbsp;mrad, and a collection angle between 86–200&nbsp;mrad. Before imaging, the specimens were baked at 160&nbsp;°C for 8&nbsp;h under vacuum to reduce surface contamination.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8518-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs8518-sec-0130-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>Standard two-electrode CR2032 coin cells were used to evaluate the electrochemical performance of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> material for SIBs and LIBs, in which NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> material was employed as working electrode and Na or Li foils as counter and reference electrodes for SIBs or LIBs, respectively. To prepare a slurry of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> for casting of the working electrodes, as-prepared NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> powder, carbon black (Super P, Imerys), and poly(vinylidene fluoride) (PVDF, average M<sub>w</sub> ≈534 000 by GPC, Sigma–Aldrich) dissolved in N-methyl-2-pyrrolidinone (NMP, 99.5%, Acros Organics, Extra Dry over Molecular Sieve) solvent were mixed thoroughly. The mass ratio of NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i>:carbon black:PVDF was 8:1:1. The NGC-Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<i><sub>x</sub></i> working electrodes were prepared by drop-casting the slurry on the carbon-coated Cu foils, then dried at 60&nbsp;°C in the oven overnight. The electrode mass loading was controlled at 1.2–1.8&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>, and we used glass fiber (GF/D, Whatman) separators. 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DEC with 1:1 ratio (by volume) and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> LiPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/ethyl methyl carbonate (EMC) with 3:7 (by mass) were used as electrolytes for SIBs and LIBs, respectively. The assembling process was conducted in an Ar-filled glovebox with O<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>O levels below 0.1&nbsp;ppm. CV and GCD were conducted using a BioLogic VMP3 electrochemical workstation and Landt CT2001A cycler, respectively. The cut-off electrochemical voltage windows of 0.001–3.0&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> or Li/Li<sup>+</sup> were used for SIB and LIB, respectively. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy with frequency from 100&nbsp;kHz–10&nbsp;mHz was conducted in a BioLogic VMP3 electrochemical workstation.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="advs8518-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="advs8518-sec-0140-title"> Computational Methods</h4>
                  
                  <p>DFT calculations were performed using the Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0045_R_d38803402e2468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Phys. Rev. B">45</a>, <a href="#advs8518-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0046_R_d38803402e2468" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Phys. Rev. B">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interaction between valence electrons and the core was&nbsp;described by the projector augmented wave (PAW) method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0047_R_d38803402e2473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Phys. Rev. B">47</a>, <a href="#advs8518-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0048_R_d38803402e2473" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Phys. Rev. B">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electronic wave functions were expanded by the plane-wave basis sets. The Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) functional of generalized gradient approximation was used for electron&nbsp;exchange-correlation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0046_R_d38803402e2478" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Phys. Rev. B">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The kinetic energy cut-off was set to 500&nbsp;eV for the plane waves. The Brillouin zone was sampled using Γ-centered 6×10×&nbsp;6 and 2×2×2 k-point meshes for the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> supercell and NGC/Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> heterostructure, respectively. To account for van der Waals interactions between MXene layers, we used Grimme's DFT&nbsp;+&nbsp;D3 method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#advs8518-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="advs8518-bib-0049_R_d38803402e2497" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Chem. Phys.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The lattice parameters and atomic positions were fully relaxed until energy and force converged at 10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;eV and 0.01&nbsp;eV Å<sup>−1</sup>, respectively.</p>
                  
                  <p>The supercell of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> with an orthogonal lattice was constructed first and its optimized lattice constants are <i>a</i> = 10.42&nbsp;Å; <i>b</i> = 6.02&nbsp;Å, and <i>c</i> = 9.67&nbsp;Å. Then, the heterostructure of NGC/Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> was constructed based on the structure of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub>. In the constructed NGC/Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> structure, the NGC layer contains 15 C atoms and 1 N atom, while the Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> layer contains 36 Ti atoms. The ratio of C in NGC to Ti is ≈0.42, which matches the experimental result of 0.36. The optimized lattice constants of NGC/Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> are&nbsp;<i>a</i> = 10.40&nbsp;Å, <i>b</i> = 8.92&nbsp;Å, and <i>c</i> = 12.34&nbsp;Å.</p>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">Based on the total energies of the NGC/Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> and its various intercalation configurations, the voltage profile for NGC/Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> electrode at 0&nbsp;K with Li<sup>+</sup> or Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation was&nbsp;determined by the following equation:

      <section id="openResearch" class="article-section article-section__open-research">
